and landlord, so [now there is conflict between capitalist and
vectoralist.]{.underline} **[Capital is dead; long live the vectoralist
class.]{.underline}** It was [with new forces of production]{.underline}
that [Capital defeated labor]{.underline} in the late twentieth century.
Like the sorcerer's apprentice, [Capital summoned]{.underline} up
[forces it could not]{.underline} restrain or [control]{.underline}.
Capital in turn finds itself struggling against a rising class that
provided the very means of that victory. [If one can use an information
infrastructure to route around labor's power to block the production
process, one can]{.underline} use the same means to [make capitalist
producers compete with each other on a global scale]{.underline}.6

#### The new class antagonism subordinates previous class relations

**Wark 19** (McKenzie Wark, Activist, Writer, PhD in Communications,
Professor at the New School for Social Research and Eugene Lang College,
Culture and Media; Program Director, Gender Studies, 2019, Verso,
\"Capital is dead\", Chapter 2: Capitalism---or Worse?)//gideon

[Industrial capitalism]{.underline} was not terribly interested in
workers who think and feel. It wanted hands. It [wanted
muscle]{.underline}. It was a flesh-eating machine.
[Whatever]{.underline} disgusting and terrifying [power lurks
in]{.underline} these more [recent stories does not so much eat bodies
as brains]{.underline}. This combinatory works two ways: [either your
mind is erased and your body is another mind's vehicle; or your mind is
subordinated to the will of another power]{.underline}. 9 Either way,
**[your mind is not your own]{.underline}**. It feels like some vile
takeover. But what if [this isn't just a takeover, but **a whole new
class relation**]{.underline}? Let's start thinking through this curious
class relation by being very "orthodox." [Let's start with]{.underline}
the forces of production, the relations of production that correspond to
them, [the class antagonism generated out of those relations of
production, and the political and culture superstructures that
correspond to that base]{.underline}.10 [And]{.underline} let us also
try to [describe]{.underline}, just as Marx did, **[what may be emerging
rather than what is established.]{.underline}** [If one **starts with
what is established**, it is **easy to interpret any new
aspect**]{.underline} of the situation **[as]{.underline}** simply
[**variations** on the same essence]{.underline}. Starting with what may
be emerging provides a suitable derangement of the senses, a giddy hint
that all that was solid is melting into air. 11 The thought experiment
that might result is quite simple. Here's a sketch, to be elaborated
upon as we go: [There]{.underline} really [is something]{.underline}
qualitatively **[distinct]{.underline}** [about the forces of production
that **eat brains**, that **produce** and **instrumentalize and control
information**]{.underline}. This is because [information]{.underline}
really [does turn out to have **strange ontological
properties**]{.underline}. Making information a force of production
produces something of a conundrum within the commodity form.
[Information wants to be free but is]{.underline} everywhere [in
chains]{.underline}.12 [Information is]{.underline} no longer scarce, it
is [infinitely replicable]{.underline}, [cheap]{.underline} to store,
cheap to transmit, [and yet the]{.underline} whole [premise of the
commodity is its scarcity.]{.underline} [Information as a force of
production calls into being]{.underline} particular [relations of
production and is]{.underline} at the same time [formed by those
relations]{.underline}. In classic Marxist style, one can look here at
the evolution of legal forms.13 In the late twentieth century
"[i]{.underline}ntellectual [p]{.underline}roperty" [emerged
as]{.underline} almost [a]{.underline}n absolute [private property
right]{.underline}.14 One [that]{.underline} [makes the]{.underline}
once separate and local property forms of pat[ent, copyright, and
trademark equivalent and exchangeable]{.underline} forms of private
property. [These]{.underline} forms [need transnational legal
enforcement]{.underline}, precisely [because information is]{.underline}
such [a slippery and abstract thing]{.underline}.15 And so, like the
enclosures or the joint-stock company before it,
[i]{.underline}ntellectual [p]{.underline}roperty [law
becomes]{.underline} the form of [a new kind of relation of
production]{.underline}, [more abstract]{.underline} than its
predecessors, and one that makes not land or physical plant, but rather
[information itself, a form of private property.]{.underline} Like those
preceding forms of private property, [this]{.underline} one [crystalizes
into a class relation.]{.underline} As an absolute form of private
property, it creates **[classes of owners and nonowners of the means of
realizing its value]{.underline}**. [Land]{.underline} as private
property [gave]{.underline} rise to the two great classes of [farmer and
landlord]{.underline}. [Capital]{.underline} as private property
[gave]{.underline} rise to the two great classes of [worker and
capitalist]{.underline}. Is there **[a new class relation]{.underline}**
that [emerges **out of**]{.underline} the **[commodification of
information]{.underline}**? For this thought experiment, let's say it
[does]{.underline}. I call those classes **[the hacker class and the
vectoralist class]{.underline}**. **[The hacker class produces new
information]{.underline}**. But what is "new" information? It is
whatever intellectual property law recognizes as new. It's a strange
kind of production. Where the farmer grows crops through a seasonal
cycle and the worker stamps out repetitive units of commodities, [the
hacker has to use their time]{.underline} in a different way, [to turn
the same old information into new]{.underline}. Getting this done is not
like the seasonal repetitions of farming or the clocking-on of the
worker. [It]{.underline} [happens when it happens]{.underline},
[including]{.underline} time spent [napping or]{.underline} pulling
[all-nighters]{.underline}.16 The workplace nightmare of the worker is
having to make the same thing, over and over, against the pressure of
the clock; [the workplace nightmare of the hacker is to produce
different things]{.underline}, over and over, [against the pressure of
the clock]{.underline}. The characters of Peggy and Don in the TV series
Mad Men work as midcentury prototypes.17 It's the advertising business
during the golden years of Fordism.18 Don is a creative, struggling
within the agency with its owners to become an owner too. Peggy is a
secretary, a white collar worker, and her struggle is to become a
creative. Meanwhile, Joan is already at the top of the secretarial pool,
managing it, but wants to become an owner. As the show progresses, the
women make a little headway in this male business. By show's end, Black
women are just starting to get the secretarial jobs, but the computer
has arrived and will make some of them obsolete anyway. Like much of
bourgeois culture, it is a small business narrative, which compresses
the classes and blurs the lines between them. The prize of becoming
truly ruling class is always just out of reach. For our purposes, the
interesting part is its picture of the activities of one prototype of
the hacker class. The camera is fascinated by Don and Peggy actually
doing their jobs. Don takes long naps on his office sofa. Sometimes he
just wanders off. [The material for]{.underline} his [brilliant ad
campaigns come from]{.underline} all sorts of [incidental
sources]{.underline}. He drinks too much, tries smoking pot. The whole
office takes amphetamine shots and pulls an all-nighter, making
speed-induced creative work full of tremendous energy and really bad
decisions. Meanwhile Peggy manages [to transition from worker to
hacker]{.underline} because she actually knows something about how to
address the desires of women, but she [ends up limited and
stymied]{.underline} in all sorts of ways [by an industry that does not
know the value of her difference]{.underline}. The less popular series
Halt and Catch Fire shows us the early tech industry version of the same
set of activities, this same work that isn't quite regular work.19
[Hackers can't be managed]{.underline} like farmers or workers; they are
not the same as either class. [There's no relation between]{.underline}
the units of [labor time and the]{.underline} units of [value produced.
Something]{.underline} cooked up [on the spur of the moment might have
enormous value]{.underline}. Long hours of [slog might end
up]{.underline} being [for nothing]{.underline}. Being exempt from
routine work is not really all that glamorous in either story, as
[it]{.underline} just [brings uncertainty]{.underline}, [frustration,
pressure, and]{.underline} (for some) [madness]{.underline}.
[Both]{.underline} of these shows [hinge on the desire to]{.underline}
escape from the limits of the hacker class and [become
owners]{.underline}. That's the limit to the desire the culture industry
can admit for this class. And yet [both]{.underline} these
[shows]{.underline} portray [a continual treadmill of hope and
failure]{.underline}. Like the farmer and the worker, [the hacker does
not]{.underline} usually [end up owning the product]{.underline} of her
efforts. Unless you own a drug company or a tech company or media
conglomerate, [you have to sell the rights to what you
produce]{.underline}. It is not always the same as selling labor power.
You might still own the intellectual property, for example. But the
hacker rarely captures much of the value of what they create or invent.
Nobody else gets to be Google's Sergey Brin precisely because there is a
Sergey Brin, who is not the avatar of the hacker class, but of its
opposite--- [the vectoralist class]{.underline}. He is the real unicorn:
the hacker become owner. The one that [perpetuates the myth that drives
a million start-ups]{.underline} on the path to the same desire, not
realizing that it is the very thing that now blocks that desire. It is
highly unlikely that your start-up will be the next Google. At best, you
might sell it to Google or to some other avatar of the vectoralist
class. **[The vectoralist class owns and controls the
vector]{.underline}**, a concept I use to describe in the abstract
**[the infrastructure on which information is routed]{.underline}**,
whether through time or space.20 A vector in geometry is simply a line
of fixed length but of unfixed position. It's a way of thinking about a
technology as having something about it that shapes the world in a
particular way, but which can shape different aspects of the world. [You
can own]{.underline} stocks or flows of [information]{.underline}, [but
far better to own the]{.underline} vector, the legal and technical
[protocols for making]{.underline} otherwise [abundant information
scarce]{.underline}. If one takes a look at [the top]{.underline}
Fortune 500 [companies]{.underline}, it is surprising how many of them
[are]{.underline} really in the [information business]{.underline}. I
don't just mean the technology and telecommunication companies like
[Apple or Google or Verizon or Cisco or]{.underline} the [drug
companies]{.underline} like Pfizer. One could also think of the [big
banks as]{.underline} a subset of the vectoralist class rather than as
"[finance capital]{.underline}." They too [are in the information
asymmetry business]{.underline}. And as we learned in the 2008 crash,
even the [car companies]{.underline} are in the information
business---they [made more money from car loans than cars]{.underline}.
[The military--- industrial sector is]{.underline} also [in the
information business]{.underline}. The companies that appear to sell
actual things, like [Nike]{.underline}, [are really in the brand
business]{.underline}. [Walmart and Amazon compete with]{.underline}
different models of the [information logistics]{.underline} business.21
Even the [oil companies are in]{.underline} part at least [in the
information-about-the-geology-of-possible-oil-deposits
business]{.underline}. Perhaps **[the vectoralist class]{.underline}**
is no longer emerging. Maybe it **[is the new dominant
class]{.underline}**. One could make the case here that information was
always central to capitalism and that this is just capitalism. To some
extent, that may be the case. However, [to]{.underline} even think [that
capitalism is about information is]{.underline} a fairly recent
perspective. It ends up being a way of [retrospectively seeing
the]{.underline} whole [course]{.underline} of capitalism [in terms of
something that only emerged]{.underline} as a concept and an
instrumental reality [as one of its late products]{.underline}. The
other point to clarify here is that [there's a difference between
information as a force of production and]{.underline} information [as a
dominant force of production.]{.underline} **[The vectoralist
class]{.underline} [doesn't need to own the other forces of
production]{.underline}** any more. [Apple and Google don't]{.underline}
actually [make their own products]{.underline}. A sizable chunk of
[those they directly employ are]{.underline} not workers but
[hackers]{.underline}, people [who come up with new
information]{.underline}, whether of a technical or cultural kind[, to
be incorporated into products whose manufacture can be tendered out to a
subordinate class of capitalists.]{.underline} That might only be the
case in the overdeveloped world where I happen to live.22 Many of the
world's peoples are not even workers but still peasants who are being
turned into tenant farmers by the theft of their common land by a
landlord class. [Much of the world is also a giant
sweatshop]{.underline}. The resistance of [labor to capital is
alive]{.underline} and well in China, India, Indonesia, and Vietnam. The
[older class antagonisms have not gone away]{.underline}. [It's
just]{.underline} that there's **[a new layer on top, trying to control
them]{.underline}**. Just as the capitalist class sought to dominate the
landlord class as a subordinate ruling class, so too **[the vectoralist
class]{.underline}** tries to [**subordinate both landlords and
capitalists** by controlling the patents, the brands, the trademarks,
the copyrights,]{.underline} but more importantly [the logistics of the
information vector]{.underline}. The vector has also worked its way
throughout the production process. This was already beginning in the
so-called Fordist era. Some proposed naming it instead after the great
Japanese companies that boomed in the mid to late twentieth century,
such as Toyota and Sony. They were the ones who figured out how to
extract not just labor but also information from the labor force. It
turns out that to extract not only efficiency but also quality from
industrial labor, it is best to incorporate the information held by
those who know the labor process best---its workers.23 That there is a
hacker class at all is in part because [workers have been stripped of
the information they possess about the labor process
itself]{.underline}. In Capital, [Marx]{.underline} mostly deals with an
ideal-type political economy with two classes. But in his political
writings it is clear that he [understands social formations as
hybrids]{.underline} of combined and overlapping modes of production.24
His writing on France isn't just a grand confrontation between
proletariat and bourgeoisie; the scene looms large with farmers,
landlords, and peasants. So here I'm simply taking my cue from the
political writings and thinking a matrix of six classes, three ruling
and three subordinate. **[The dominant classes are landlords,
capitalists, vectoralists. The subordinate classes are farmers, workers,
hackers]{.underline}**. [Now]{.underline} [imagine]{.underline} all
[the]{.underline} possibilities of [class alliance and conflict that
this generates]{.underline}. It turns out that [politics is]{.underline}
much less about the relation between the friend and the enemy, and much
more crucially [about relations among nonfriends and
nonenemies]{.underline}.25 [It's]{.underline} about [shifting alliances
of]{.underline} [convenience between heterogeneous class
interests]{.underline}. It's about [conflicts]{.underline} that can
[take many forms,]{.underline} only some of them open,
[many]{.underline} of them [discreet]{.underline}.

### AT: Other Ks

#### Stop talking about 'subversion' and 'revolution' -- that game's over. You're playing into capitalist realism -- the theological obsession with capital-as-eternal, that it must be negated or subsumed. The world's materiality is critical but so is the lens through which we view it, and that lens has been dirtied by submission and recreation of the same essence through modified modifiers. We must understand the world beyond capital, something worse, with a new approach and rhetoric. Old systems of thought and writing are stuck in the 19^th^ century

**Wark 19** (McKenzie Wark, Activist, Writer, PhD in Communications,
Professor at the New School for Social Research and Eugene Lang College,
Culture and Media; Program Director, Gender Studies, 2019, Verso,
\"Capital is dead\", Chapter 1: The Sublime Language of My
Century)//gideon

[One thing that the left and right]{.underline} [now seem to
agree]{.underline} on [is that the society in which we live
is]{.underline} called [capitalism]{.underline}.1 And strangely enough,
[both]{.underline} now [seem to agree that it is eternal]{.underline}.
[Even the left seems to think that there is an eternal essence to
Capital]{.underline} [and]{.underline} that [only its appearances
change]{.underline}. [The parade of changing appearances
yields]{.underline} a series of [modifiers]{.underline}: this could be
[necro]{.underline} capitalism, [communicative]{.underline} capitalism,
[cognitive]{.underline} capitalism, [platform]{.underline} capitalism,
[neoliberal]{.underline} capitalism, [or computational]{.underline}
capitalism.2 But short of an increasingly allegorical or messianic leap
into something other, it is as if [this]{.underline}
self-[same]{.underline} [thing]{.underline} just [went on
forever]{.underline}. I have a taste for the writerly tactics of
modernism, so whenever I come across a piece of language about which
there is such wide consensus I want to trouble it somehow. 3
[This]{.underline} capitalism that we have all agreed that we live in,
[has]{.underline} it not [become too]{.underline} familiar, too
[cozy]{.underline}, too roomy an idea? [Why are we so
devoted]{.underline} to its name? The reality the term tried to describe
is, of course, far from comfortable. [Capitalism is a world of
exploitation, domination, and oppression.]{.underline}
[Capitalism]{.underline}, if this is what this still is,
[appears]{.underline} to be [like a steam-hammer smashing]{.underline}
not only the social but also the natural conditions of its [existence to
pieces]{.underline}. But then maybe this is the thing to ask about. [Why
have we become so comfortable]{.underline} with a way of [describing an
uncomfortable reality]{.underline}? Do we want a certainty in language
that can't be had anywhere else? That the world we live in is capitalism
has become a familiar way of describing something that destroys what is
familiar. 4 [Capitalism atomizes and alienates]{.underline}. It renders
everything precarious---except its own hold on the imagination. If the
greatest trick of the devil was to persuade us that the devil does not
exist, then maybe [the **greatest trick of capitalism** is to gull us
into **imagining that there is nothing but eternal
capitalism**]{.underline}. It is hard to describe [things]{.underline}
that [change imperceptibly]{.underline}. 5 Some changes are like the
crack in the china cup that just appears one day. 6 [This
may]{.underline} well [be the]{.underline} level of
[language]{.underline} on which the problem rests. [Language has to
describe change using]{.underline} the [combinations]{.underline} and
permutations [of terms]{.underline} that language offers: **[the
combinatory]{.underline}**. [This combinatory]{.underline} of terms
always [has]{.underline} something of [a binary quality]{.underline}. 7
If this is not capitalism, well then it must be communism, the term that
negates it. [Since this is]{.underline} obviously [not
communism]{.underline}, then [it must]{.underline} still [be
capitalism]{.underline} after all. But what about when the change to be
described doesn't correspond well to the neat digital chop between one
term and another? Perhaps [it is as hard to describe
transitions]{.underline} between modes of production as it is to
describe changes in mood. There was once a language about transitions
between modes of production. There's an elaborate argument about how
feudalism became capitalism, about whether there might be multiple
routes toward capitalism, about whether there could be more than one
kind of socialism to come after. The debates about where capitalism came
from are fascinating but mostly of academic interest.8 The debates about
where it might go got caught up in Cold War discourse; with the demise
of the Soviet Union, they appear to be moot. [With the truncating of the
historical time line to]{.underline} [the chunk in the middle called
capitalism]{.underline}, [the **historical imagination finds itself
reduced**]{.underline} as well. That [language]{.underline} tends to
work this way [leaves us with a very odd situation]{.underline}. Now,
both the left and the right alike end up working within the same
language about this being capitalism. It was surely not Marx's intention
that the language he brought together to get critical leverage on his
times would become commonplace terms also used by our enemies. Among
other qualities as a writer, [Marx]{.underline} really [was one of the
great modern poets]{.underline}.9 He made modifications to the language
that have stuck. Of course he worked with the materials of the languages
he had at hand, but he wrought something lasting: a combinatory of
terms, a matrix of concepts, for describing History. 10 Like any great
poetic corpus, his work contains multitudes. But a few standard
permutations came to stick in the mind, like great pop songs, although
maybe with misremembered lyrics.11 Here I think is [his greatest
hit]{.underline}, his epic track, the one that has become something of
an earworm. Here's how it [goes]{.underline}: [this is
capitalism]{.underline}. It has an essence and it has appearances. [Its
appearances are false]{.underline}, a phantasmagoria of fetishes, in
which [commodities appear]{.underline} as if endowed [with]{.underline}
self-moving [spirit]{.underline}. Its real essence is defined by these
things: [the commodity form]{.underline}, [with]{.underline} its doublet
of [use]{.underline} value [and exchange value]{.underline}; by labor's
double form, [as concrete labor and abstract labor]{.underline}; by [the
extraction of surplus value]{.underline} in the production process, by
[the wage relation]{.underline}, by the rising [organic]{.underline}
composition of [capital]{.underline}, in which more and more of it is
made up of [dead labor]{.underline} rather than living labor, by [the
crisis]{.underline} caused of the tendency of the rate of [profit to
fall]{.underline}. And finally, [by negation]{.underline}.12 One could
debate endlessly whether this is what Marx really meant, but I think
that's a fair condensation of how many have heard him. It's a sort of
ur-version of [Marx]{.underline} that has [become]{.underline} something
of a refrain. Or even a [myth]{.underline}. [There are]{.underline}
actually [two main variants of the myth]{.underline} here [about
negation]{.underline}. [Either capitalism negates itself]{.underline},
brought to ruin [by its own contradictions. Or it is negated by a
subject]{.underline} that it produces as its own negation, [the working
class]{.underline}. In either variant, one thing is key: [until the
moment of negation]{.underline}, [capitalism can change its appearances
but never its essence]{.underline}. **[Its essence can only be negated
by contradiction or struggle]{.underline}**. Assorted variant tunes
spill out of this rhetorical frame, mutating like genres of techno
music. [There are]{.underline} other [ways to perform variations on
Marx's combinatory]{.underline} of terms. For instance, one can
[swap]{.underline} out the abstract verb [negation]{.underline}
[and]{.underline} replace it with [acceleration]{.underline}. This
approach was popular again in the early twentyfirst century, as it was
in the early twentieth century. 13 Here the idea is that there's nothing
that can negate capital, either in its own contradictions or in the
force it produces in and against itself. Rather, the best one can do is
[accelerate it to its end]{.underline}, toward a Promethean leap into
another mode of production.14 But note that this is not as much a change
in tune as its advocates like to imagine. [It leaves]{.underline} intact
[the mythic form of Capital as an essence]{.underline}. Yet [faith in
either the negation or acceleration of Capital has grown
faint]{.underline}. [The essence of Capital is
eternal]{.underline}---this is the striking feature of how it is now
imagined.15 Naturally, [those who]{.underline} love it [embrace
this]{.underline} thought. It needs merely to be perfected by our love.
This [is]{.underline} sometimes called (with a stunning lack of
imagination) [neoliberalism]{.underline}. But what is even stranger is
that [those who do not love it seem to agree]{.underline}.16 The essence
of Capital is eternal. [It goes on forever]{.underline}, and [everything
is an expression of its essence]{.underline}. Capital is the essence
expressed everywhere, and its expression is tending to become ever more
total. The other side of the eternal essence of Capital is its
ever-changing appearances. [Change is]{.underline} accounted for
[through]{.underline} the use of [modifiers]{.underline}. Its
appearances can even be periodized. [There was merchant capitalism, then
liberal capitalism, then monopoly capitalism, then neoliberal
capitalism]{.underline}. (Let's not even mention that other and more
problematic category, [the Asiatic mode of production]{.underline},
because that [was not supposed to have a history]{.underline}. 17)
There's some ambiguity as to what to call [the current
s]{.underline}tage, however. It [could be disaster, cognitive, semio,
neuro, late, biopolitical, neoliberal, or postfordist
capitalism]{.underline}, to name just a few options.18 Note that [the
last two are temporal modifications to a modifier: neoliberal,
postfordist]{.underline}. [Could there be any better tribute to the
**complete enervation of the imaginal faculty by capitalism**, or

**[Modify the modifier]{.underline}**? Besides adding modified modifiers
to the sacred category of Capital, another variant is worth a mention,

[poetics]{.underline} that [opens a split]{.underline} within its

[overboard with the binary difference]{.underline} between two terms

[Capitalism]{.underline} itself [is about ideas]{.underline}. It's

[immaterial labor of the overdeveloped world happen without an

little book is thus a provocation: to think the possibility that
[**capitalism has** already **been** **rendered historical**
but]{.underline} that [**the period that replaces it is
worse**.]{.underline} [That]{.underline} it could be worse [gets us away
from the happy narratives in which latter-day capitalism is the magic

propose to write [the present]{.underline} a[s]{.underline} including
**[a new kind of class conflict]{.underline}**, [including]{.underline}
**[new kinds of class,]{.underline}** arising out of recent mutations in

[see]{.underline} the outlines of [the present afresh]{.underline},
**[estranged from our habits of thought]{.underline}**. There was once a
grand attempt to have done with at least part of this great epic-poetic

[Nowhere]{.underline} in these tunes [is]{.underline} there that

[defamiliarization]{.underline} when the roof falls in.29 One has to

[vigorous attempts to tell a different story]{.underline}, to strike a
different tune, [were made in bad faith]{.underline}. There was a time
when it was a popular art form. While the Soviet Union claimed ownership
of the narrative of capitalism and its coming negation, you could make a
good living in the "free world" coming up with a different story. Not
surprisingly, it was former Marxists and socialists who wrote most of
those alternative epic poems that sprouted into whole worldviews. These
former Marxists would sing of the glories of the "managerial
revolution," of the "postindustrial society," of the conditions for
"take-off" and growth, of the "future shock" of technological
disruption. What these [epic narratives]{.underline} all had in common
was that they [**accepted the basic Marxist combinatory of terms** for
understanding History. They conceded its power, its poetry]{.underline}.
31 [But]{.underline} they [changed the ending]{.underline}. Rather than
negation, the story ends with Capital resolving its own contradictions.
It's a happy ending that Theodor Adorno would have called an extorted
reconciliation?32 This mytho-poetics had some currency during the Cold
War. But [with the]{.underline} [collapse of the]{.underline} supposedly
socialist world of the [Soviet Union]{.underline}, which claimed all
subsidiary rights to the great Marxist story, these [counternarratives
lost their force]{.underline}. One influential counterstory from the
twentieth century survives. The author who inspired it, Joseph
[Schumpeter]{.underline}, was not a socialist, although he briefly
worked for a socialist government.33 He probably [got it
from]{.underline} that original Marxist sellout, Werner
[Sombart]{.underline}. [In this play]{.underline} on the combinatory of
Marxist language, Ca[pital affirms itself continually by negating itself
continually]{.underline}. [It negates itself, and in an affirmative way,
as "creative destruction." It can "disrupt" itself]{.underline}! Indeed,
[its essence becomes its selfdisruption]{.underline}. And **[it is our
sacred duty never to get in its way]{.underline}**. [In our own times
this]{.underline} old [story was adapted into]{.underline} the belief
system of the so-called tech industry, as a part of what Richard
Barbrook calls **[the California Ideology]{.underline}**. 34 Into it can
be folded certain other variations, about **[the "fourth industrial
revolution]{.underline}**," for example.35 The conceit of all these
postcapitalist stories was that [this is not the]{.underline} same [old
capitalism---it's better!]{.underline} When people hear the beginnings
of a story about this no longer being capitalism, their resistance
generally rises. Unless you happen to be worth several million dollars,
the chances are you do not perceive this as something better than
capitalism or a capitalism that always improves on itself. Maybe it
would be interesting, aesthetically and politically, to take the other

that this is not capitalism, it's better, what if we explored the line

too. This I can tell you from experience, having tried to write

certainty of the devil they know]{.underline}, or think they know, [for
something that promises to be worse.]{.underline} So the bad news is:
**[this is not capitalism anymore, it's something worse]{.underline}**.
And the good news is: **[Capital is not eternal, and even if this mode
of production is worse, it is not forever. There could be others. That's
the struggle today]{.underline}**. OK, so that's not particularly good
news. But [there is]{.underline} also [this]{.underline}: [an end to
left-melancholia]{.underline}, [that eternal sadness about eternal
capitalism]{.underline}.37 Interestingly, [few people will even attempt
to think Capital-is-dead even as a thought experiment.]{.underline}
[There]{.underline} really [is something fundamental to the myth that
this is capitalism, **as if Capital were the name of a
God**]{.underline}. It may even be the defining feature of ideology
today. Ideology today is not the acceptance of a neoliberal structure of
feeling or habits of thought and action.38 [Ideology today is clinging
to the belief that this is capitalism]{.underline}. To think [that we

[might concede too much]{.underline} reality [to the belief in eternal
Capital]{.underline}.39 I think **[it's time to be bold]{.underline}**.
Let's [reanimate Marx's infamous remark: "All I know is that I am not a
Marxist]{.underline}."40 What if we took that in the sense that he was
not one of those who simply took a language and a poetic form extracted
from his predecessors as a given? [He]{.underline} was, to the contrary,
the one who had [constructed]{.underline} that [language with
a]{.underline} quite [particular purpose]{.underline} in mind: [to
understand the situation of his times from the labor point of
view]{.underline}. So: what if we kept the commitment to understanding,
not his situation, but ours, from the labor point of view---whatever
that might mean now---and bracketed off the rest? That makes a certain
sense to me. [I really am puzzled by why we should use blocks of
linguistic material from his time to understand our time]{.underline}.
**[Why use Marx's]{.underline}** playful [modifications of
the]{.underline} fashionable philosophy, the [popular
science]{.underline}, the political tracts, [or the **technological
metaphors of the midnineteenth century**]{.underline}? [When
poets]{.underline} or novelists [inhabit old forms]{.underline} like
that, [we]{.underline} immediately [think it's dated or ironically
retro]{.underline}. But [somehow **we want our critical theory to still
be about eternal Capital, as if it were some subgenre of
steampunk**]{.underline}.41 Different genres of text have a different
relationship to tradition and innovation, and at different moments in
their development. They aren't always in synch. [There's]{.underline}
generally [a **culture industry** that **pulps**]{.underline} the more
[**innovative texts into sameness** and an avant-garde trying to escape
that sameness and do something else]{.underline}. If you are trying to
write an interesting (rather than merely successful) novel or poem, you
want to change things at the formal level, rather than ship your wine in
the same old bottles. The thing is, [where readings and rewritings of
Marx are concerned]{.underline}, [they seem to]{.underline} me to
[belong to the culture industry]{.underline}. [It's a
commonplace]{.underline} now [to read **Capital as a work of
philosophy**]{.underline} or even as a novel, but to do so **[with a
distinctly un-Marxist reverence]{.underline}**.42 Like everything else,
the transmission of the Marxian corpus through time is a matter of what
Raymond Williams called selective tradition.43 [Most textology of Marx
deploys]{.underline} conventional protocols of [quotation, exegesis, and
interpretation]{.underline}.44 In these habitual readings, [selections
from the **canonic texts are made to yield an underlying meaning that
subtends them**.]{.underline} The texts count as evidence that
represents an underlying essence. Where Capital is thought as an essence
that produces appearances that are false, [the **Marx-corpus is read as
an appearance that is true to an essence**]{.underline} ---most of the
time. Marx's texts can be discreetly corrected to correspond to their
true essence. (Writing this sort of Marx fan-fiction gives our
conservative textologists enough of a thrill of originality). This
essence is the veracity against which the false appearances of the world
are then held to account. Alternatively, rather than read the
Marx-corpus through the interpretive filter of a Marx essence, [Marx
texts can be read through the interpretive filter of someone else's
text]{.underline}. This yields all of the supplemental Marxisms: Marx
read [through structuralism]{.underline}, [psychoanalysis,
deconstruction, and so on]{.underline}.45 Rather than a Marx-corpus read
in terms of its fidelity to a Marxessence that it resembles, [this
procedure]{.underline} is a bit different. It [yields a Marx who says
something other than what he probably thought he meant to
say]{.underline}. [There's still a "real" Marx, to be interpreted, but
it may be at variance with the surface of the text.]{.underline}
Capitalism can then be read in terms of what this other Marx meant, and
the surface effects of Marx that don't conform to it are themselves
residues of Capital itself. Pursued to its limit, this method tends to
become post-Marxist, when Marx himself appears to be more symptom than
diagnosis.46 One ends up saying, with Foucault: "[Marxism exists in the
nineteenth century like a fish in water: that is, unable to breathe
anywhere else]{.underline}."47 The paradox here is that because the
reading protocols operating in the selective tradition of Marxism are
rather conservative, [Marx ends up snagged on the language of his
times]{.underline}. His combinatory is not really opened up to play
freely in our times.

#### Ideology is a myth -- information infrastructure caused change, not an idea -- reject their historical idealism

**Wark 19** (McKenzie Wark, Activist, Writer, PhD in Communications,
Professor at the New School for Social Research and Eugene Lang College,
Culture and Media; Program Director, Gender Studies, 2019, Verso,
\"Capital is dead\", Chapter 5: A Time Machine Theory of
History)//gideon

[The **myth of neoliberalism** is that **the idea**]{.underline} of
neoliberalism **[came first]{.underline}**, [and then]{.underline}
**[politicians]{.underline}** like Margaret Thatcher and Ronald Reagan
[**made it policy** and then law.]{.underline} [This narrative
is]{.underline} sometimes popular among leftists despite its clearly
**[idealist]{.underline}** view of history. 48 I think it's possible to
tell the story another way. After all, [what made it possible to
implement neoliberal policies]{.underline} in the first place? [What
changed]{.underline} since the seventies that made it possible [to
**globalize banking** and build vast **international supply chains** to
combine components of a manufacturing process from all over the
world]{.underline}? The clue is already there in the stray fact that
Microsoft came into existence in the mid-seventies. [It was not
information as an idea]{.underline} ---free markets---[that changed the
mode of production]{.underline}. [It was **a vast, global infrastructure
in which information enabled the control of flows of money, machines,
resources, and labor.**]{.underline} [If you can use a **computer to**
calculate the positions of ten thousand atoms in a protein, you
can]{.underline} use it to **[calculate a global production system that
routes]{.underline}** around [**the power of**]{.underline} militant
**[labor]{.underline}** in a factory in Detroit.49 There isn't really a
time machine that will take you back to the seventies. Or rather, we
have only a one-way time machine, or perhaps not a time machine so much
as a tome machine. [You can **look in the archive** for some neglected
storylines]{.underline}, and the past comes back as something else.
Maybe something even more amazing than the surprises you could spring on
people in the past if you had a time machine are the surprises the past
can spring on us through the tome machine of the archive. [Maybe we
could practice a]{.underline} kind of [historical art]{.underline}, of
telling the stories otherwise, as a way of inquiring into why certain
kinds of story are neglected or suppressed. [The default
stories]{.underline} selected from the combinatory of story elements
[may be **arbitrary narrative habits**]{.underline}. [Here's a
story]{.underline}, then: **[It is an error to call our times neoliberal
when its politics are not "neo" and its politics are not
"liberal,"]{.underline}** anyway. [The politics of the present
might]{.underline} just as well [be described]{.underline} with the
equally retro term **[alt-fascist]{.underline}**.50 [It is]{.underline}
all [about]{.underline} **[securing ruling class power]{.underline}**
[through the manipulation o]{.underline}f [**racial and ethnic
prejudice** and the use of **surveillance** and **overt violence** to
**suppress dissent**]{.underline}. [It is]{.underline} centrally [about
the **prison--- industrial complex**]{.underline}**, [expanded now on a
global scale]{.underline},** as Angela Davis reminds us.51 [What is
**new** is **not the politics**]{.underline} at all, [which is a
**farcical double of the superstructures of old**]{.underline},
[but]{.underline} rather **[the mode of production underneath
it]{.underline}**. Here one might say that the [economics are **not
"liberal**"]{.underline} either and **[that is what makes them
new]{.underline}**. **[Forces of production organized around information
change the commodity form.]{.underline}**

### AT: Tech Always Good / Bad

#### The form of technology is capital -- it is the dead labor of our time. There is no inherent normative being to technology; only its use for and by capital is wrong

**Wark 19** (McKenzie Wark, Activist, Writer, PhD in Communications,
Professor at the New School for Social Research and Eugene Lang College,
Culture and Media; Program Director, Gender Studies, 2019, Verso,
\"Capital is dead\", Chapter 3: The Forces of Production)//gideon

[The first and last question]{.underline} that usually comes up [about
technology is whether it is]{.underline} a [good or a bad]{.underline}
thing. This can apply both to particular technologies and to technology
in general. [Marx can]{.underline} readily [be]{.underline} [recruited
to either side]{.underline} of this argument, either through quoting
selectively, picking the Marx of a particular period, or extracting a
particular perspective out of his work at the expense of the dialectical
and poetical play at work in his corpus. Marx has a lot of uses on the
technology question. He is an allpurpose tool. Marx might also offer
"tools" for thinking a bit more critically about technology. Do we have
to subject technology to a moral decision only, as good or bad? How does
this technology appear as a thing apart that one could contemplate and
judge? From what kind of genteel point of view does it seem something
separate? What range of things are we thinking of as technical, anyway?
Starting from the last of those questions[, it is important to situate
Marx in his own times.]{.underline} [What we now think of]{.underline}
as [technology was for Marx]{.underline} [more a question of **the
machine**]{.underline}. [His was an era of steam]{.underline}, which
powered factories, railways, shipping, and the printing presses of the
newspapers for which he wrote.1 It was an era of telegraphy, but
[before]{.underline} the wide distribution of [electric power,
or]{.underline} the rise of [modern chemistry, particle physics,
genetics, climate science, or information science]{.underline}. Marx did
his best to keep up with the scientific and technical developments of
his time, but that was more Engels's job. [Marx's]{.underline} knowledge
of how the physical world works, and hence his [materialism, stops short
at a certain historical threshold]{.underline}.2 Nevertheless, Marx
makes important steps toward thinking technology as a set of things that
can be grasped with a concept. Particularly in the Grundrisse[, he
starts to write about **technology beyond the moral decision of whether
it is good or bad**]{.underline}.3 He starts to write of it as [having a
**range of possibilities**]{.underline}, as something that has, as one
might say in a more modern idiom, "[affordances]{.underline}."
Consistent with the rest of his thinking, [Marx comes to understand
technology **not as having an essence**]{.underline}, [but]{.underline}
as something [emerging out of]{.underline} particular [historical
circumstances.]{.underline} This gave him a way to think beyond the
curious way that [technology appears as something separate]{.underline}.
Technology is [intimately connected]{.underline} on the one hand [to the
human and]{.underline} on the other to the [nonhuman]{.underline}.
Indeed, [technology may be **the inhuman zone**]{.underline} [where
distinctions between]{.underline} the [human and]{.underline} the
[nonhuman]{.underline}, not to mention anxieties about their
permeability, [originate]{.underline}. Among other things, technology
mediates senses of the human to the human. To take up just the first of
these connections for now: Marx could see technology as connected to the
human in a double way. To put it simply: **[the content of technology is
labor; the form of technology is capital]{.underline}**. It is **[living
labor]{.underline}** that [**makes** **technology**]{.underline};
**[technology is congealed dead labor]{.underline}**---pink goo
---[that]{.underline} then **[returns to confront the worker in the form
of capital]{.underline}**. The form of [technology]{.underline} is
capital, in that it [is shaped by]{.underline} the objective of
[**extracting value** from labor (and from nature) **as** **efficiently
as possible.** As]{.underline} particular [capitalist firms
compete]{.underline} with each other, [they reach for labor-saving
devices to increase output and drive down costs]{.underline},
**[replacing living labor with dead labor]{.underline}**,
[but]{.underline} in the long run [putting a squeeze on
profits]{.underline}, [as surplus value is extracted]{.underline} from
exploiting [human labor alone]{.underline}. The form of technology is
capital in a second sense, too. [Not all decisions that capital
makes]{.underline} about technical change in the workplace
[are]{.underline}, strictly speaking, [economic]{.underline}. [Capital
may also implement technical change that takes power away from the
worker]{.underline} at the point of production.4
[Technology]{.underline} is not a separate thing, then. It [is intimate
to the human]{.underline}, in a bifurcated way: **[capital in form is
capital; in content, it is labor]{.underline}**. **[Labor makes the
machine, but not in the design of its own choosing]{.underline}**. There
is a parallel connection, on the other side, to the nonhuman, to nature.
[Technology is made of, and remakes, nature itself]{.underline}.
[Technology's content is]{.underline} sensuous
[materiality]{.underline}, iron and coal and so forth, [mixed with
labor]{.underline}; [its form is]{.underline} once again [the form of
capital]{.underline}. Rimbaud: "**[If the brass awakens as a horn, it's
not to blame]{.underline}**."5

#### The internet's a liberating tool but was coopted by the California ideology to create the new ruling class in the '90s. The 'hero' of the entrepreneur hides a history of oppression and violence. Whether technology is good or bad cannot be found without first resolving class conflicts

**Wark 19** (McKenzie Wark, Activist, Writer, PhD in Communications,
Professor at the New School for Social Research and Eugene Lang College,
Culture and Media; Program Director, Gender Studies, 2019, Verso,
\"Capital is dead\", Chapter 3: The Forces of Production)//gideon

[The Internet came out of the university and took capital by
surprise]{.underline}. While many a scholar was writing genteel Marxist
theory in the humanities, in information science [a whole new mode of
production was germinating]{.underline}. It had already transformed much
of the sciences. By the nineties [there was enough cheap
technology]{.underline} around [to]{.underline} take a punk approach and
[develop new theories and practices]{.underline} with and about it. I'll
mention just two examples, starting with Dmytri Kleiner's détourned
text, the Telekommunist Manifesto.36 Kleiner's starting point is the
transformation of the forces of production and the pressure this put on
the relations of production. [**Cheap computation plus the Internet**
vector was supposed to **make capitalism more efficient** and enable
capital to **route around the power of workers** at the point of
production]{.underline}. It did all that, but [it also opened the
prospect of **self-organized peer-to-peer production**]{.underline}.37
[There]{.underline} really [could have been a
**telecommunism**]{.underline} ("tele" = "at a distance"). [Autonomous
producers could cheaply and easily communicate and
coordinate]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} was a possibility that [had
to be foreclosed to enable]{.underline} a new kind of capture of the
surplus by the rising ruling class that I call [the vectoralist
class]{.underline}. On the technical level, [what developed in place of
a peer-to-peer network]{.underline} [was a **client--server
network**]{.underline}, built around privately owned hubs--- what
Benjamin Bratton calls stacks.38 Meanwhile, [states]{.underline} engaged
each other in trade agreements, which [produced transnational regimes of
intellectual property]{.underline} designed [to **secure surplus
information** within novel forms of private property]{.underline}. The
free creation of [information would be]{.underline} alternately policed
and encouraged: [policed where it infringed on corporate
monopolies]{.underline}; [encouraged where free labor]{.underline} or
[nonlabor could be captured as information that had value]{.underline}.
We now have the information commons as a form of disintegrated
spectacle, owned by the vectoralist class. [What Kleiner advocates as a
counterstrategy is]{.underline} what he calls **[venture
communism]{.underline}**: "Politics is not a battle of ideas it is a
battle of capacities."39 [The hacker class has to **create its own
autonomous forms**]{.underline}. "A specter is haunting the net, the
specter of communism." 40 Like Kleiner, Richard Barbrook (and his
collaborator, the late Andy Cameron) emerged out of the intersection of
media activism and practice and produced illuminating détournements of
Marxist texts as a way to grasp the nineties situation. [Barbrook took
the point of]{.underline} view of what he called [digital artisans
against the rise of the virtual class]{.underline}.41 What was
particularly useful in these polemical texts was the identification of
[the California Ideology as the worldview of this emerging ruling

that [even critics of "bad technology" take it for gospel.]{.underline}
The California Ideology emerged out of seemingly progressive movements
of the counterculture in California in the mid to late twentieth
century. 42 Once again, repression played a role. [Black

job]{.underline}. Shorn of its more radical edge, [the counterculture

posture made]{.underline} its [peace with free market libertarian

which technology itself was the sole transformative force of history. 44
[The hero]{.underline} of this epical-poetical myth [was the

is [good in essence]{.underline}. Barbrook follows Marx in seeking out

notes [the irony of the retro-futurist celebration of]{.underline}

that [fails to mention that he was a slaveholder]{.underline}. 46
Unfortunately, many of the tech-pundits of today lack Barbrook's wit and
historical acumen, and they take much of the California Ideology for
granted. The result is a sort of conspiracy theory, in which the public
was allegedly duped by a cabal of "Silicon Valley" entrepreneurs. [To
mask their intention to unleash]{.underline} powerful tools of [monopoly
and political control, they lulled everyone into thinking technology was
in essence good when it is in essence bad.]{.underline} As Barbrook
points out, the development of the forces of production is not magically
called into being from the brains of business geniuses. In the case of
Silicon Valley, [it took a massive amount of state funding, passing
through university research labs]{.underline}.47 It may at one point
have been quite possible that [these developments could have led to a
**digital agora**]{.underline} or commons as well as (or as an
alternative to) new forms of class power based on information
asymmetries and the surveillance state. [What gets erased in **both

is good and the one where its essence is bad, [is the **struggle over

would take. [Both]{.underline} Barbrook and Kleiner [get critical

[replacing them with a language saturated in the emerging class

[and]{.underline} Barbrook's [cyber]{.underline}communism. [I read
this]{.underline} now [as a useful transitional tactic for working in
and against the combinatory of terms inherited from former historical

[wherein]{.underline} the development of the [forces]{.underline} of
